<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Nuclear power</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Atomic power" redirects here. For the film, see <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Power_(film)" title="Atomic Power (film)">Atomic Power (film)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2377" data-file-width="3328" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/220px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/330px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg/440px-2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2011-05-10_18-57-46_Switzerland_-_Wil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MWe" title="MWe">MWe</a> <a href="/wiki/Leibstadt_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Leibstadt Nuclear Power Plant">Leibstadt Nuclear Power Plant</a> in Switzerland. The <a href="/wiki/Boiling_water_reactor" title="Boiling water reactor">boiling water reactor</a> (BWR), located inside the dome capped cylindrical structure, is dwarfed in size by its <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling tower</a>. The station produces a yearly average of 25 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hours</a> per day, sufficient to power a city the size of <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/220px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/330px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg/440px-PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PaloVerdeNuclearGeneratingStation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Palo_Verde_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Palo Verde Nuclear Generating Station">Palo Verde Nuclear Generating Station</a>, the largest in the US with 3 <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactors</a> (PWRs), is situated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arizona_desert" title="Arizona desert">Arizona desert</a>. It uses <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> from cities as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooling_water" title="Cooling water">cooling water</a> in 9 squat mechanical draft cooling towers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Its total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spent_fuel" title="Spent fuel">spent fuel</a>/"waste" inventory produced since 1986, is contained in <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> cylinders located between the artificial body of water and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_switchyard" title="Electrical switchyard">electrical switchyard</a>.</div></div></div>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>2016 World [civil] power generation by source [IEA, 2018] (Percentages of 24.973 TWh)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA-Report-keyworld-2018_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-Report-keyworld-2018-4">[4]</a></sup>

<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; </div>
</div></div>
<p><b>Nuclear power</b> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a> that release <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear energy</a> to generate heat, which most frequently is then used in <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbines</a> to produce electricity in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a>. 
Nuclear power can be obtained from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. 
Presently, the vast majority of electricity from nuclear power is produced by nuclear fission of elements in the <a href="/wiki/Actinide#Applications" title="Actinide">actinide</a> series of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>.
Nuclear decay processes are used in niche applications such as <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a>.
The possibility of generating electricity from nuclear fusion is still at a research phase with no commercial applications.
This article mostly deals with nuclear fission power for electricity generation.
</p><p>Nuclear power is one of the leading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power_generation" title="Low carbon power generation">low carbon power generation</a> methods of producing <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>.
In terms of <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">total life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions per unit of energy generated</a>, nuclear power has emission values comparable or lower than <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nrel.gov_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nrel.gov-5">[5]</a></sup>
From the beginning of its commercialization in the 1970s, nuclear power prevented about 1.84 million air pollution-related deaths and the emission of about 64 billion tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalent</a> that would have otherwise resulted from the burning of fossil fuels in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Civilian nuclear power supplied 2,488 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt_hour" title="Terawatt hour">terawatt hours</a> (TWh) of electricity in 2017, equivalent to about 10% of global electricity generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaea_pris_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaea_pris-7">[7]</a></sup>
As of April 2018, there are <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">449 civilian fission reactors in the world</a>, with a combined electrical capacity of 394 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> (GW). 
Additionally, there are 58 reactors under construction and 154 reactors planned, with a combined capacity of 63 GW and 157 GW, respectively. Over 300 more reactors are proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-8">[8]</a></sup>
Most of reactors under construction are of <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">generation III reactor</a> design, with the majority in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaea_pris_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-iaea_pris-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a social <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">debate about nuclear power</a>.
Proponents, such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmentalists_for_Nuclear_Energy" title="Environmentalists for Nuclear Energy">Environmentalists for Nuclear Energy</a>, contend that nuclear power is a safe, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">sustainable energy</a> source that reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a>.
<a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Opponents</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenpeace_International" title="Greenpeace International">Greenpeace International</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Information_and_Resource_Service" title="Nuclear Information and Resource Service">NIRS</a>, contend that nuclear power poses many threats to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_radioactivity" title="Environmental radioactivity">people and the environment</a>.
</p><p>Far-reaching <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">fission power reactor accidents</a>, or accidents that resulted in medium to long-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_product" title="Fission product">fission product</a> contamination of inhabited areas, have occurred in <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">Generation I and II reactor</a> designs. 
These include the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> in 1986, the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> in 2011, and the more contained <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> in 1979.
There have also been some nuclear submarine accidents.
In terms of lives lost per unit of energy generated, fission-electric reactors have caused the lowest number of fatalities per unit of energy generated when compared to the other major energy producing methods.
Coal, petroleum, natural gas and hydroelectricity each have caused a greater number of fatalities per unit of energy, due to air pollution and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accident</a> effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Collaboration on research and development towards greater safety, efficiency and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">recycling of spent fuel</a> in future <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">Generation IV reactors</a> presently includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euratom" title="Euratom">Euratom</a> and the co-operation of more than 10 permanent countries globally.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="671" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/220px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/330px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg/440px-Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Binding_energy_curve_-_common_isotopes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">Nuclear binding energy</a> of all natural elements in the periodic table. With higher values translating into more tightly bound nuclei, the greatest nuclear stability. <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Iron</a>(Fe), is both the end product of <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> within the core of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> fusing stars and the production of elements surrounding iron are likewise the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_products" title="Fission products">fission products</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinides" title="Actinides">fissionable actinides</a>(e.g uranium). Iron is also seen as the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_stability" title="Valley of stability">trough</a> rather than the peak of the graph as shown, were all other elemental nuclei have the potential to be nuclear fuel, much like "a ball rolls down a hill <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_stability" title="Valley of stability">to the valley floor</a>", with the greater numerical separation or "height" difference from iron, the greater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_potential_energy" title="Nuclear potential energy">nuclear potential energy</a> that could be released.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission#History" title="Nuclear fission">Nuclear fission § History</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Age" title="Atomic Age">Atomic Age</a></div>
<p>In 1932 physicist <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> discovered that when lithium atoms were "split" by protons from a proton accelerator, immense amounts of energy were released in accordance with the principle of <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">mass–energy equivalence</a>. However, he and other nuclear physics pioneers <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> believed harnessing the power of the atom for practical purposes anytime in the near future was unlikely, with Rutherford labeling such expectations "moonshine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The same year, his doctoral student <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> which was immediately recognized as a potential tool for nuclear experimentation because of its lack of an electric charge. Experimentation with bombardment of materials with neutrons led <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Frédéric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène Joliot-Curie</a> to discover <a href="/wiki/Induced_radioactivity" title="Induced radioactivity">induced radioactivity</a> in 1934, which allowed the creation of radium-like elements at much less the price of natural radium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Further work by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a> in the 1930s focused on using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slow_neutron" title="Slow neutron">slow neutrons</a> to increase the effectiveness of induced radioactivity. Experiments bombarding uranium with neutrons led Fermi to believe he had created a new, transuranic element, which was dubbed <a href="/wiki/Hesperium" title="Hesperium">hesperium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Np_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Np-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1938, German chemists <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Fritz Strassmann</a>, along with Austrian physicist <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and Meitner's nephew, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Robert_Frisch" title="Otto Robert Frisch">Otto Robert Frisch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> conducted experiments with the products of neutron-bombarded uranium, as a means of further investigating Fermi's claims. 
They determined that the relatively tiny neutron split the nucleus of the massive uranium atoms into two roughly equal pieces, contradicting Fermi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Np_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Np-13">[13]</a></sup> 
This was an extremely surprising result: all other forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_decay" title="Nuclear decay">nuclear decay</a> involved only small changes to the mass of the nucleus, whereas this process—dubbed "fission" as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">reference to biology</a>—involved a complete rupture of the nucleus. 
Numerous scientists, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3_Szil%C3%A1rd" title="Leó Szilárd">Leó Szilárd</a>, who was one of the first, recognized that if fission reactions released additional neutrons, a self-sustaining <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a> could result. Once this was experimentally confirmed and announced by Frédéric Joliot-Curie in 1939, scientists in many countries (including the United States, the United Kingdom, France, Germany, and the Soviet Union) petitioned their governments for support of nuclear fission research, just on the cusp of World War II, for the development of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_nuclear_reactor">First nuclear reactor</span></h3>
<p>In the United States, where Fermi and Szilárd had both emigrated, the discovery of the nuclear chain reaction led to the creation of the first man-made reactor, known as <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Pile-1" title="Chicago Pile-1">Chicago Pile-1</a>, which achieved <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">criticality</a> on December 2, 1942. 
This work became part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, a massive secret U.S. government military project to make <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a> and by building large production reactors to produce (<i>breed</i>) <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> for use in the first nuclear weapons. 
The United States would test an atom bomb in July 1945 with the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity test</a>, and eventually two such weapons were used in the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="500" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/220px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/330px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/440px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first light bulbs ever lit by electricity generated by nuclear power at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EBR-1" title="EBR-1">EBR-1</a> at <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory-West</a>, December 20, 1951.</div></div></div>
<p>In August 1945, the first widely distributed account of nuclear energy, in the form of the pocketbook <i>The Atomic Age</i>, discussed the peaceful future uses of nuclear energy and depicted a future where fossil fuels would go unused. 
Nobel laureate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glenn_Seaborg" title="Glenn Seaborg">Glenn Seaborg</a>, who later chaired the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a>, is quoted as saying "there will be <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_thermal_rocket" title="Nuclear thermal rocket">nuclear powered earth-to-moon shuttles</a>, nuclear powered <a href="/wiki/Artificial_heart" title="Artificial heart">artificial hearts</a>, plutonium heated swimming pools for SCUBA divers, and much more".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Kingdom, Canada,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and the USSR proceeded to research and develop nuclear industries over the course of the late 1940s and early 1950s. 
Electricity was generated for the first time by a nuclear reactor on December 20, 1951, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EBR-I" title="EBR-I">EBR-I</a> experimental station near <a href="/wiki/Arco,_Idaho" title="Arco, Idaho">Arco, Idaho</a>, which initially produced about 100 kW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> 
Work was also strongly researched in the United States on nuclear marine propulsion, with a test reactor being developed by 1953 (eventually, the <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS Nautilus</a>, the first nuclear-powered submarine, would launch in 1955).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> 
In 1953, American President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> gave his "<a href="/wiki/Atoms_for_Peace" title="Atoms for Peace">Atoms for Peace</a>" speech at the United Nations, emphasizing the need to develop "peaceful" uses of nuclear power quickly. This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_Act_of_1954" title="Atomic Energy Act of 1954">1954 Amendments to the Atomic Energy Act</a> which allowed rapid declassification of U.S. reactor technology and encouraged development by the private sector.
</p><p>The controllability of nuclear power reactors depends on the fact that a small fraction of neutrons resulting from fission are <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_physics#Delayed_neutrons_and_controllability" title="Nuclear reactor physics">delayed</a>, which makes the reactions easier to control. 
These are neutrons emitted by the decay of certain <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission_product" title="Nuclear fission product">fission products</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
Operating in this delayed critical state, changes in reaction rates occur slowly enough to permit mechanical feedback to control those reaction rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span></h3>
<p>On June 27, 1954, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Obninsk_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant">Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant</a> became the world's first nuclear power plant to generate electricity for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_grid" title="Power grid">power grid</a>, and produced around 5 megawatts of electric power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEANews_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEANews-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA2-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later in 1954, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Strauss" title="Lewis Strauss">Lewis Strauss</a>, then chairman of the United States Atomic Energy Commission (U.S. AEC, forerunner of the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a>) spoke of electricity in the future being "<a href="/wiki/Too_cheap_to_meter" title="Too cheap to meter">too cheap to meter</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thisdayinquotes_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-thisdayinquotes-27">[27]</a></sup> Strauss was very likely referring to hydrogen fusion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> —which was secretly being developed as part of <a href="/wiki/Project_Sherwood" title="Project Sherwood">Project Sherwood</a> at the time—but Strauss's statement was interpreted as a promise of very cheap energy from nuclear fission. The U.S. AEC itself had issued far more realistic testimony regarding nuclear fission to the U.S. Congress only months before, projecting that "costs can be brought down... [to]... about the same as the cost of electricity from conventional sources..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1955 the United Nations' "First Geneva Conference", then the world's largest gathering of scientists and engineers, met to explore the technology. In 1957 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EURATOM" title="EURATOM">EURATOM</a> was launched alongside the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (the latter is now the European Union). The same year also saw the launch of the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA).
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_(11823864155).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2558" data-file-width="3200" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/220px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/330px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg/440px-Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_%2811823864155%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station_(11823864155).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Calder Hall, United Kingdom – The world's first commercial nuclear power station. First connected to the national power grid on 27 August 1956 and officially opened by Queen Elizabeth II on 17 October 1956</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shippingport_Reactor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="516" data-file-width="662" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/220px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/330px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Shippingport_Reactor.jpg/440px-Shippingport_Reactor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shippingport_Reactor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Shippingport_Atomic_Power_Station" title="Shippingport Atomic Power Station">Shippingport Atomic Power Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shippingport,_Pennsylvania" title="Shippingport, Pennsylvania">Shippingport, Pennsylvania</a> was the first commercial reactor in the United States and was opened in 1957.</div></div></div>
<p>The world's first commercial nuclear power station, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calder_Hall_nuclear_power_station" title="Calder Hall nuclear power station">Calder Hall</a> at Windscale, England, was opened in 1956 with an initial capacity of 50 MW (later 200 MW).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kragh_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kragh-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc17oct_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc17oct-31">[31]</a></sup> The first commercial nuclear generator to become operational in the United States was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shippingport_Reactor" title="Shippingport Reactor">Shippingport Reactor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, December 1957).
</p><p>One of the first organizations to develop nuclear power was the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a>, for the purpose of propelling <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>. The first nuclear-powered submarine, <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS <i>Nautilus</i></a>, was put to sea in December 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaeapdf_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaeapdf-32">[32]</a></sup> As of 2016, the U.S. Navy submarine fleet is made up entirely of nuclear-powered vessels, with 75 submarines in service. Two U.S. nuclear submarines, <a href="/wiki/USS_Scorpion_(SSN-589)" title="USS Scorpion (SSN-589)">USS <i>Scorpion</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Thresher_(SSN-593)" title="USS Thresher (SSN-593)">USS <i>Thresher</i></a>, have been lost at sea. As of 2016 the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Navy" title="Russian Navy">Russian Navy</a> was estimated to have 61 nuclear submarines in service; eight Soviet and Russian nuclear submarines have been lost at sea. This includes the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-19" title="Soviet submarine K-19">Soviet submarine <i>K-19</i></a> reactor accident in 1961 which resulted in 8 deaths and more than 30 other people were over-exposed to radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rad_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-rad-33">[33]</a></sup> 
The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-27" title="Soviet submarine K-27">Soviet submarine <i>K-27</i></a> reactor accident in 1968 resulted in 9 fatalities and 83 other injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnston2007_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-34">[34]</a></sup> 
Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-429" title="Soviet submarine K-429">Soviet submarine <i>K-429</i></a> sank twice, but was raised after each incident. Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_by_death_toll" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents by death toll">serious nuclear and radiation accidents</a> have involved nuclear submarine mishaps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timenuke_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnston2007_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a> also had a <a href="/wiki/Army_Nuclear_Power_Program" title="Army Nuclear Power Program">nuclear power program</a>, beginning in 1954. The SM-1 Nuclear Power Plant, at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Belvoir" title="Fort Belvoir">Fort Belvoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, was the first power reactor in the United States to supply electrical energy to a commercial grid (VEPCO), in April 1957, before Shippingport. The <a href="/wiki/SL-1" title="SL-1">SL-1</a> was a U.S. Army experimental nuclear power reactor at the <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory" title="Idaho National Laboratory">National Reactor Testing Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Idaho" title="Eastern Idaho">eastern Idaho</a>. It underwent a <a href="/wiki/Steam_explosion" title="Steam explosion">steam explosion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_meltdown" title="Nuclear meltdown">meltdown</a> in January 1961, which killed its three operators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In the Soviet Union at The <a href="/wiki/Mayak" title="Mayak">Mayak</a> Production Association facility there were a number of accidents, including an explosion, that released 50–100 tonnes of high-level radioactive waste, contaminating a huge territory in the eastern Urals and causing numerous deaths and injuries. The Soviet government kept this accident secret for about 30 years. The event was eventually rated at 6 on the seven-level INES scale (third in severity only to the disasters at <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_accident" title="Fukushima nuclear accident">Fukushima</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG/220px-Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG/330px-Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG/440px-Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Satsop_Development_Park_07780.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Public_Power_Supply_System" title="Washington Public Power Supply System">Washington Public Power Supply System</a> Nuclear Power Plants 3 and 5 were never completed.</div></div></div>
<p>Installed nuclear capacity initially rose relatively quickly, rising from less than 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">gigawatt</a> (GW) in 1960 to 100 GW in the late 1970s, and 300 GW in the late 1980s. Since the late 1980s worldwide capacity has risen much more slowly, reaching 366 GW in 2005. Between around 1970 and 1990, more than 50 GW of capacity was under construction (peaking at over 150 GW in the late 1970s and early 1980s) — in 2005, around 25 GW of new capacity was planned. More than two-thirds of all nuclear plants ordered after January 1970 were eventually cancelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaeapdf_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-iaeapdf-32">[32]</a></sup> A total of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_canceled_nuclear_plants_in_the_United_States" title="List of canceled nuclear plants in the United States">63 nuclear units were canceled</a> in the United States between 1975 and 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1970s and 1980s rising economic costs (related to extended construction times largely due to regulatory changes and pressure-group litigation)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and falling fossil fuel prices made nuclear power plants then under construction less attractive. In the 1980s (U.S.) and 1990s (Europe), flat load growth and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electricity_liberalization" title="Electricity liberalization">electricity liberalization</a> also made the addition of large new baseload capacity unattractive.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> had a significant effect on countries, such as France and Japan, which had relied more heavily on oil for electric generation (39%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and 73% respectively) to invest in nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some local opposition to nuclear power emerged in the early 1960s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-well_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-well-41">[41]</a></sup> and in the late 1960s some members of the scientific community began to express their concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfgang_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-42">[42]</a></sup> These concerns related to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents and incidents">nuclear accidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">high cost of nuclear power plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a> and <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">radioactive waste disposal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bm_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-bm-43">[43]</a></sup> In the early 1970s, there were large protests about a proposed nuclear power plant in <a href="/wiki/Wyhl" title="Wyhl">Wyhl</a>, Germany. The project was cancelled in 1975 and anti-nuclear success at Wyhl inspired opposition to nuclear power in other parts of Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pub_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-pub-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-got_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-got-45">[45]</a></sup> By the mid-1970s anti-nuclear activism had moved beyond local protests and politics to gain a wider appeal and influence, and nuclear power became an issue of major public protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jimfalk_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-jimfalk-46">[46]</a></sup> Although it lacked a single co-ordinating organization, and did not have uniform goals, the movement's efforts gained a great deal of attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eleven_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-47">[47]</a></sup> In some countries, the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">nuclear power conflict</a> "reached an intensity unprecedented in the history of technology controversies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marcuse.org_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-marcuse.org-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ANTIAKW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1416" data-file-width="2232" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ANTIAKW.jpg/220px-ANTIAKW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ANTIAKW.jpg/330px-ANTIAKW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/ANTIAKW.jpg/440px-ANTIAKW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ANTIAKW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>120,000 people attended an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_protest" title="Anti-nuclear protest">anti-nuclear protest</a> in Bonn, Germany, on October 14, 1979, following the <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kits_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-kits-49">[49]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In France, between 1975 and 1977, some 175,000 people protested against nuclear power in ten demonstrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kits_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-kits-49">[49]</a></sup> In West Germany, between February 1975 and April 1979, some 280,000 people were involved in seven demonstrations at nuclear sites. Several site occupations were also attempted. In the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> in 1979, some 120,000 people attended a demonstration against nuclear power in <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kits_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-kits-49">[49]</a></sup> In May 1979, an estimated 70,000 people, including then governor of California <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Brown" title="Jerry Brown">Jerry Brown</a>, attended a march and rally against nuclear power in Washington, D.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diablo_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-diablo-50">[50]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_anti-nuclear_power_groups" title="List of anti-nuclear power groups">Anti-nuclear power groups</a> emerged in every country that has had a nuclear power programme.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_Mile_Island_and_Chernobyl">Three Mile Island and Chernobyl</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="2240" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/220px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/330px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/440px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The abandoned city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pripyat_(city)" title="Pripyat (city)">Pripyat</a> with Chernobyl plant in the distance.</div></div></div>
<p>Health and safety concerns, the 1979 <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">accident at Three Mile Island</a>, and the 1986 <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> played a part in stopping new plant construction in many countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolfgang_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-wolfgang-42">[42]</a></sup> although the public policy organization, the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> states that new nuclear units, at the time of publishing in 2006, had not been built in the United States because of soft demand for electricity, and <a href="/wiki/Cost_overrun" title="Cost overrun">cost overruns</a> on nuclear plants due to regulatory issues and construction delays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tbi_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-tbi-51">[51]</a></sup> By the end of the 1970s it became clear that nuclear power would not grow nearly as dramatically as once believed. Eventually, more than 120 reactor orders in the United States were ultimately cancelled<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and the construction of new reactors ground to a halt. A cover story in the February 11, 1985, issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine commented on the overall failure of the U.S. nuclear power program, saying it "ranks as the largest managerial disaster in business history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike the Three Mile Island accident, the much more serious Chernobyl accident did not increase regulations affecting Western reactors since the Chernobyl reactors were of the problematic <a href="/wiki/RBMK" title="RBMK">RBMK</a> design only used in the Soviet Union, for example lacking "robust" <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment buildings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC-54">[54]</a></sup> Many of these RBMK reactors are still in use today. However, changes were made in both the reactors themselves (use of a safer enrichment of uranium) and in the control system (prevention of disabling safety systems), amongst other things, to reduce the possibility of a duplicate accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>An international organization to promote safety awareness and professional development on operators in nuclear facilities was created: <a href="/wiki/World_Association_of_Nuclear_Operators" title="World Association of Nuclear Operators">World Association of Nuclear Operators</a> (WANO).
</p><p>Opposition in Ireland and Poland prevented nuclear programs there, while Austria (1978), Sweden (1980) and Italy (1987) (influenced by Chernobyl) voted in referendums to oppose or phase out nuclear power. In July 2009, the Italian Parliament passed a law that cancelled the results of an earlier referendum and allowed the immediate start of the Italian nuclear program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> 
After the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> a one-year moratorium was placed on nuclear power development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> followed by a referendum in which over 94% of voters (turnout 57%) rejected plans for new nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_renaissance">Nuclear renaissance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OL3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="2813" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/250px-OL3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/375px-OL3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/OL3.jpg/500px-OL3.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OL3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olkiluoto_3" title="Olkiluoto 3">Olkiluoto 3</a> under construction in 2009. It is the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Pressurized_Reactor" title="European Pressurized Reactor">EPR</a> design, but problems with workmanship and supervision have created costly delays which led to an inquiry by the Finnish nuclear regulator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%A4teilyturvakeskus" title="Säteilyturvakeskus">STUK</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnn161009_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-wnn161009-59">[59]</a></sup> In December 2012, Areva estimated that the full cost of building the reactor will be about €8.5 billion, or almost three times the original delivery price of €3 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNN-2012-07-17_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNN-2012-07-17-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-2012-07-16_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-2012-07-16-62">[62]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner noresize" style="width:302px;"><div class="thumbimage" style="width:300px;"><div style="margin: 15px 5px -40px -50px; font-size: 80%;"><div class="chart noresize" style="margin-top:1em;max-width:300px;">
<div style="position:relative;min-height:200px;min-width:300px;max-width:300px;">
<div style="float:right;position:relative;min-height:120px;min-width:200px;max-width:200px;border-left:1px black solid;border-bottom:1px black solid;">
<div style="position:absolute;left:1px;top:29px;height:90px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:blue;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;border:1px solid blue;border-bottom:none;overflow:hidden;" title="Generation (TWh): 2,263.79"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:10px;top:28px;height:91px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:blue;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;border:1px solid blue;border-bottom:none;overflow:hidden;" title="Generation (TWh): 2,298.27"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:19px;top:25px;height:94px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:blue;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;border:1px solid blue;border-bottom:none;overflow:hidden;" title="Generation (TWh): 2,378.93"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:28px;top:22px;height:97px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:blue;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;border:1px solid blue;border-bottom:none;overflow:hidden;" title="Generation (TWh): 2,443.85"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;left:37px;top:20px;height:99px;min-width:5px;max-width:5px;background-color:blue;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;border:1px solid blue;border-bottom:none;overflow:hidden;" title="Generation (TWh): 2,511.09"></div>

</div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">Operational nuclear reactors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRISstatsglobal_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-PRISstatsglobal-63">[63]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_renaissance" title="Nuclear renaissance">Nuclear renaissance</a></div>
<p>Since about 2001 the term <i>nuclear renaissance</i> has been used to refer to a possible nuclear power industry revival, driven by rising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" title="Price of petroleum">fossil fuel prices</a> and new concerns about meeting <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emission limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-intro-64">[64]</a></sup> 
Since commercial nuclear energy began in the mid-1950s, 2008 was the first year that no new nuclear power plant was connected to the grid, although two were connected in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tf2010_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-tf2010-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Disaster">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Disaster</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant</a></div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">Tōhoku earthquake</a> on 11 March 2011, one of the largest earthquakes ever recorded, and a subsequent tsunami off the coast of Japan, the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant">Fukushima Daiichi Nuclear Power Plant</a> suffered multiple core meltdowns due to failure of the emergency cooling system for lack of electricity supply. This resulted in the most serious nuclear accident since the Chernobyl disaster.
</p><p>The Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident prompted a re-examination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety">nuclear safety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">nuclear energy policy</a> in many countries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-67">[67]</a></sup> and raised questions among some commentators over the future of the renaissance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
Germany approved plans to close all its reactors by 2022, and Italy re-affirmed its ban on nuclear power in a referendum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-67">[67]</a></sup> 
China, Switzerland, Israel, Malaysia, Thailand, United Kingdom, and the Philippines reviewed their nuclear power programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piersmorgan.blogs.cnn.com_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-piersmorgan.blogs.cnn.com-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.xinhuanet.com_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.xinhuanet.com-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011 the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> halved its prior estimate of new generating capacity to be built by 2035.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist-20110428_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist-20110428-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-late_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-late-75">[75]</a></sup> 
Nuclear power generation had the biggest ever fall year-on-year in 2012, with nuclear power plants globally producing 2,346 TWh of electricity, a drop of 7% from 2011. 
This was caused primarily by the majority of Japanese reactors remaining offline that year and the permanent closure of eight reactors in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna13_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna13-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Fukushima">Post-Fukushima</span></h3>
<p>The Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident sparked controversy about the importance of the accident and its effect on nuclear's future. 
The crisis prompted countries with nuclear power to review the safety of their reactor fleet and reconsider the speed and scale of planned nuclear expansions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platts_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Platts-77">[77]</a></sup> 
In 2011, <i>The Economist</i> opined that nuclear power "looks dangerous, unpopular, expensive and risky", and that "it is replaceable with relative ease and could be forgone with no huge structural shifts in the way the world works".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ2011_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-econ2011-78">[78]</a></sup> 
Earth Institute Director Jeffrey Sachs disagreed, claiming combating climate change would require an expansion of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
Investment banks were also critical of nuclear soon after the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2011, German engineering giant <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> announced it will withdraw entirely from the nuclear industry as a response to the Fukushima accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC18Sep2011_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC18Sep2011-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2012, the United States Nuclear Regulatory Commission approved the construction of two additional reactors at the <a href="/wiki/Vogtle_Electric_Generating_Plant" title="Vogtle Electric Generating Plant">Vogtle Electric Generating Plant</a>, the first reactors to be approved in over 30 years since the Three Mile Island accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yah12_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-yah12-84">[84]</a></sup>
In October 2016, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watts_Bar_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Watts Bar Nuclear Generating Station">Watts Bar 2</a> became the first new United States reactor to enter commercial operation since 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013 Japan signed a deal worth $22 billion, in which <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Heavy_Industries" title="Mitsubishi Heavy Industries">Mitsubishi Heavy Industries</a> would build four modern <i><a href="/wiki/Atmea" title="Atmea">Atmea</a></i> reactors for Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> 
In August 2015, following 4 years of near zero fission-electricity generation, Japan began restarting its nuclear reactors, after <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster#Equipment.2C_facility_and_operational_changes" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">safety upgrades were completed</a>, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Sendai_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Sendai Nuclear Power Plant">Sendai Nuclear Power Plant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 2015, the IAEA's outlook for nuclear energy had become more promising. 
"Nuclear power is a critical element in limiting greenhouse gas emissions," the agency noted, and "the prospects for nuclear energy remain positive in the medium to long term despite a negative impact in some countries in the aftermath of the [Fukushima-Daiichi] accident...it is still the second-largest source worldwide of low-carbon electricity. 
And the 72 reactors under construction at the start of last year were the most in 25 years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
According to the World Nuclear Association, as of 2015 the global trend was for new nuclear power stations coming online to be balanced by the number of old plants being retired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2015, <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">441 reactors</a> had a worldwide net electric capacity of 382,9 GW, with 67 new nuclear reactors under construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MyUser_Https:_May_22_2016c_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-MyUser_Https:_May_22_2016c-90">[90]</a></sup>
Over half of the 67 total being built were in Asia, with 28 in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, where there is an urgent need to control pollution from coal plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
Eight new grid connections were completed by China in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNN20160307_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNN20160307-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_of_the_industry">Future of the industry</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_prospective_nuclear_units_in_the_United_States" title="List of prospective nuclear units in the United States">List of prospective nuclear units in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_the_United_States" title="Nuclear power in the United States">Nuclear power in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" title="Mitigation of global warming">Mitigation of global warming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ulchin_(now_Hanul)_04790182_(8506930230).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="3216" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg/220px-Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg/330px-Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg/440px-Ulchin_%28now_Hanul%29_04790182_%288506930230%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ulchin_(now_Hanul)_04790182_(8506930230).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Hanul_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Hanul Nuclear Power Plant">Hanul nuclear power station</a> in South Korea, presently the second largest in the world by output, with six operating power reactors, by 2023 it was planned to eventually consist of ten operating reactors, when two additional and indigenously designed <a href="/wiki/APR-1400" title="APR-1400">APR-1400</a> reactors on site, that are presently under construction, similarly received government approval and constructon commenced over the period of 2018-2022. In 2012 the Korean designer exported the APR design to the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Barakah_nuclear_power_plant" title="Barakah nuclear power plant">four of the Gen III reactors</a> are under construction.</div></div></div>
<p>As of 2018, there are over 150 nuclear reactors planned including 50 under construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> However, while investment on upgrades of existing plant and life-time extensions continues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnn-20180717_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-wnn-20180717-95">[95]</a></sup> investment in new nuclear is declining, reaching a 5-year-low in 2017. 
</p><p>In 2015, the International Energy Agency reported that the Fukushima accident had a strongly negative effect on nuclear power, yet nuclear power prospects are positive in the medium to long term mainly thanks to new construction in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
In 2016, the U.S. Energy Information Administration projected for its “base case” that world nuclear power generation would increase from 2,344 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatt_hour" title="Terawatt hour">terawatt hours</a> (TWh) in 2012 to 4,501 TWh in 2040. 
Most of the predicted increase was expected to be in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The future of nuclear power varies greatly between countries, depending on government policies. 
Some countries, many of them in Europe, such as Germany, Belgium, and Lithuania, have adopted policies of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_phase-out" title="Nuclear power phase-out">nuclear power phase-out</a>. 
At the same time, some Asian countries, such as China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> and India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> have committed to rapid expansion of nuclear power. 
Many other countries, such as the United Kingdom<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and the United States, have policies in between. 
Japan generated about 30% of its electricity from nuclear power before the Fukushima accident.
In 2015 the Japanese government committed to the aim of restarting its fleet of 40 reactors by 2030 after safety upgrades, and to finish the construction of the Generation III <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Cma_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Ōma Nuclear Power Plant">Ōma Nuclear Power Plant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> 
This would mean that appoximately 20% of electricity would come from nuclear power by 2030.
As of 2018, some reactors have restarted commercial operation following inspections and upgrades with new regulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> 
While <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_South_Korea" title="Nuclear power in South Korea">South Korea has a large nuclear power industry</a>, the new government in 2017, partly influenced by a large anti-nuclear movement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> committed to halting nuclear development and to gradually phase out nuclear power as reactors that are now operating or under construction close after 40 years of operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nei-20180130_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-nei-20180130-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wnn-20170619_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-wnn-20170619-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The nuclear power industry in western nations have a history of construction delays, cost overruns, plant cancellations, and nuclear safety issues, despite significant government subsidies and support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jk_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-jk-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greeninc_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-greeninc-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rb_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-rb-108">[108]</a></sup>
These problems are related to very strict safety requirements, uncertain regulatory environment, slow rate of construction, and large stretches of time with no nuclear construction and consequent loss of know-how.
Commentators therefore argue that nuclear power is impractical in western countries because of high costs, popular opposition, and regulatory uncertainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmc_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-jmc-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Quiggin_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Quiggin-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannah_Northey_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hannah_Northey-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lowenuke_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-lowenuke-112">[112]</a></sup>
Recent financial problems of western nuclear companies, most prominently the bankruptcy of <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Company" title="Westinghouse Electric Company">Westinghouse</a> in March 2017 because of US$9 billion of losses from nuclear construction projects in the United States, suggest a shift to the East, as the dominant exporter and designer of nuclear fuel and reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nei-20170329_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-nei-20170329-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bl-20170330_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-bl-20170330-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The greatest new build activity is occurring in Asian countries like South Korea, India and China. 
In March 2016, China had 30 reactors in operation, 24 under construction and plans to build more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAChina_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNAChina-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuclear_Power_in_China_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nuclear_Power_in_China-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BN-800" title="BN-800">BN-800</a> sodium cooled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" title="Fast reactor">fast reactor</a> in Russia, began commercial electricity generation, while plans for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BN-1200" title="BN-1200">BN-1200</a> were initially conceived the future of the fast reactor program in Russia awaits the results from MBIR, an under construction multi-loop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generation_IV" title="Generation IV">Generation IV</a> research facility for testing the chemically more inert lead, lead-<a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_cooled_fast_reactor" title="Gas cooled fast reactor">gas coolants</a>, it will similarly run on recycled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOX" title="MOX">MOX</a> (mixed uranium and plutonium oxide) fuel. An on-site <a href="/wiki/Pyroprocessing" title="Pyroprocessing">pyrochemical processing</a>, closed fuel-cycle facility, is planned, to reduce the necessity for a growth in uranium mining and exploration. In 2017 the manufacture program for the reactor commenced with the facility open to collaboration under the "International Project on Innovative Nuclear Reactors and Fuel Cycle", it has a construction schedule, that includes an operational start in 2020. As planned, it will be the world's most-powerful research reactor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, licenses of almost half of the operating nuclear reactors have been extended to 60 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world-nuclear_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-world-nuclear-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-matt2010_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-matt2010-120">[120]</a></sup> 
The U.S. NRC and the U.S. Department of Energy have initiated research into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor_sustainability" title="Light water reactor sustainability">Light water reactor sustainability</a> which is hoped will lead to allowing extensions of reactor licenses beyond 60 years, provided that safety can be maintained, to increase energy security and preserve low-carbon generation sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LifeAfter60_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-LifeAfter60-121">[121]</a></sup> 
Research into nuclear reactors that can last 100 years, known as <a href="/wiki/Centurion_Reactor" title="Centurion Reactor">Centurion Reactors</a>, is being conducted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r2_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-r2-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a>, globally during the 1980s one new nuclear reactor started up every 17 days on average, and in the year 2015 it was estimated that this rate could in theory eventually increase to one every 5 days, although no plans exist for that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_power_plants">Nuclear power plants</span></h2>

<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">Nuclear power plant</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">List of nuclear reactors</a></div>
<p>Just as many conventional <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a> generate electricity by harnessing the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> released from burning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>, nuclear power plants convert the energy released from the nucleus of an atom via nuclear fission that takes place in a nuclear reactor. The heat is removed from the reactor core by a cooling system that uses the heat to generate steam, which drives a <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> connected to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">generator</a> producing electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAnuclearreactorbasics_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNAnuclearreactorbasics-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>A fission nuclear power plant is generally composed of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>, in which the nuclear reactions generating heat take place; a cooling system, which removes the heat from inside the reactor; a <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>, which transforms the heat in <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_energy" title="Mechanical energy">mechanical energy</a>; an <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">electric generator</a>, which transform the mechanical energy into electrical energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAnuclearreactorbasics_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNAnuclearreactorbasics-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Installed_capacity_and_electricity_production">Installed capacity and electricity production</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">Nuclear power by country</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_reactors" title="List of nuclear reactors">List of nuclear reactors</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_percentage.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Nuclear_power_percentage.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_percentage.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_percentage.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of electricity produced by nuclear power in the world</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_station.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Nuclear_power_station.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_station.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_station.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The status of nuclear power globally (click image for legend)</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="967" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/220px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/330px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg/440px-Annual_electricity_net_generation_in_the_world.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Net <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generation" title="Electrical generation">electrical generation</a> by source and growth from 1980 to 2010. (Brown) – fossil fuels.(Red) – Fission.(Green)- "all renewables". In terms of energy generated between 1980 and 2010, the contribution from fission grew the fastest.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_power_history.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/220px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/330px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nuclear_power_history.svg/440px-Nuclear_power_history.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The rate of new construction builds for civilian fission-electric reactors essentially halted in the late 1980s, with the effects of accidents having a <a href="/wiki/Chilling_effect" title="Chilling effect">chilling effect</a>. Increased <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> realizations in existing reactors was primarily responsible for the continuing increase in electrical energy produced during this period. The halting of new builds c. 1985, resulted in greater fossil fuel generation, see above graph.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="2050" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/220px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/330px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png/440px-Top_5_Nuclear_Energy_Producing_Countries.png 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Electricity generation trends in the top five fission-energy producing countries (US EIA data)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Nuclear fission power stations, excluding the contribution from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">naval nuclear fission reactors</a>, provided 11% of the world's electricity in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd_pdf_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-oecd_pdf-125">[125]</a></sup> somewhat less than that generated by <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydro-electric stations</a> at 16%.
