Since electricity accounts for about 25% of humanity's energy usage with the majority of the rest coming from fossil fuel reliant sectors such as transport, manufacture and home heating, nuclear fission's contribution to the <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global final energy consumption</a> was about 2.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> 
This is a little more than the combined global electricity production from wind, solar, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and geothermal power, which together provided 2% of global final energy consumption in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">IAEA</a> reported that there were 437 operational civil fission-electric reactors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaea_pris_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-iaea_pris-7">[7]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">31 countries</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UIC_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-UIC-128">[128]</a></sup> although not every reactor was producing electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> 
In addition, there were approximately 140 naval vessels using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion" title="Nuclear propulsion">nuclear propulsion</a> in operation, powered by about 180 reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear power's share of global electricity production has fallen from 16.5% in 1997 to about 10% in 2017, in large part because the economics of nuclear power have become more difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft-20180903_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20180903-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regional differences in the use of nuclear power are large.
The United States produces the most nuclear energy in the world, with nuclear power providing 19% of the electricity it consumes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> while France produces the highest percentage of its electrical energy from nuclear reactors—80% as of 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr20060501_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-npr20060501-135">[135]</a></sup> 
In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> as a whole nuclear power provides 30% of the electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> 
Nuclear power is the single largest low-carbon electricity source in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-issues_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-issues-137">[137]</a></sup> and accounts for two-thirds of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>'s low-carbon electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a> differs among European Union countries, and some, such as Austria, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Italy" title="Nuclear power in Italy">Italy</a>, have no active nuclear power stations. 
In comparison, France has a large number of nuclear reactors in use and nuclear power supplied over 70% of its electricity in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many military and some civilian (such as some <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreakers</a>) ships use <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">nuclear marine propulsion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> 
A few space vehicles have been launched using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>: 33 reactors belong to the Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RORSAT" title="RORSAT">RORSAT</a> series and one was the American <a href="/wiki/SNAP-10A" title="SNAP-10A">SNAP-10A</a>.
</p><p>International research is continuing into additional uses of process heat such as <a href="/wiki/Water_splitting#Nuclear-thermal" title="Water splitting">hydrogen production</a> (in support of a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen economy</a>), for <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalinating</a> sea water, and for use in <a href="/wiki/District_heating" title="District heating">district heating</a> systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_companies_in_the_nuclear_sector" title="List of companies in the nuclear sector">List of companies in the nuclear sector</a></div>
<p>The nuclear industry consists of a number of companies, organizations, governmental and international bodies.
The main fields of the industry include nuclear reactor building and operation; <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">uranium mining</a> and nuclear fuel production; nuclear waste storage and processing; research and development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_industry_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA_industry-142">[142]</a></sup>
Other components of the nuclear industry include nuclear regulators and nuclear industry national and international associations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA_industry_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNA_industry-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of nuclear power plants">Economics of nuclear power plants</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1440" data-file-width="2560" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/220px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/330px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG/440px-Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ikata_Nuclear_Powerplant.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ikata_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Ikata Nuclear Power Plant">Ikata Nuclear Power Plant</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">pressurized water reactor</a> that cools by utilizing a secondary coolant <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchanger</a> with a large body of water, an alternative cooling approach to large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooling_towers" title="Cooling towers">cooling towers</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The economics of new nuclear power plants is a controversial subject, since there are diverging views on this topic, and multibillion-dollar investments depend on the choice of an energy source. 
Nuclear power plants typically have high capital costs for building the plant, but low fuel costs. 
Comparison with other power generation methods is strongly dependent on assumptions about construction timescales and capital financing for nuclear plants as well as the future costs of fossil fuels and renewables as well as for energy storage solutions for intermittent power sources. 
Cost estimates also need to take into account <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">plant decommissioning</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a> storage costs. 
On the other hand, measures to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" title="Mitigation of global warming">mitigate</a> <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions_trading" title="Carbon emissions trading">carbon emissions trading</a>, may favor the economics of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Analysis of the economics of nuclear power must also take into account who bears the risks of future uncertainties. 
To date all operating nuclear power plants were developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalized" title="Nationalized">state-owned</a> or <a href="/wiki/Regulated_market" title="Regulated market">regulated</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_utility" title="Electric utility">electric utility</a> monopolies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft-20100912_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20100912-145">[145]</a></sup> where many of the risks associated with construction costs, operating performance, fuel price, accident liability and other factors were borne by consumers rather than suppliers. 
In addition, because the potential liability from a nuclear accident is so great, the full cost of liability insurance is generally limited/capped by the government, which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission">U.S. Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> concluded constituted a significant subsidy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> 
Many countries have now liberalized the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_market" title="Electricity market">electricity market</a> where these risks, and the risk of cheaper competitors emerging before capital costs are recovered, are borne by plant suppliers and operators rather than consumers, which leads to a significantly different evaluation of the economics of new nuclear power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-2003_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-2003-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although nuclear power plants can vary their output, the electricity is generally less favorably priced when doing so. 
Nuclear power plants are therefore typically run as much as possible to keep the cost of the generated electrical energy as low as possible, supplying mostly base-load electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Internationally the price of nuclear plants rose 15% annually in 1970–1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> 
Yet, nuclear power has total costs in 2012 of about $96 per megawatt hour (MWh), most of which involves capital construction costs, compared with solar power at $130 per MWh, and natural gas at the low end at $64 per MWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
Following the 2011 Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster, costs are expected to increase for then-operating and new nuclear power plants, due to increased requirements for on-site spent fuel management and elevated design basis threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle_of_nuclear_fuel">Life cycle of nuclear fuel</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="923" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/220px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/330px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png/440px-Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Fuel_Cycle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The nuclear fuel cycle begins when uranium is mined, enriched, and manufactured into nuclear fuel, (1) which is delivered to a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a>. After usage in the power plant, the spent fuel is delivered to a reprocessing plant (2) or to a final repository (3) for geological disposition. In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">reprocessing</a> 95% of spent fuel can potentially be recycled to be returned to usage in a power plant (4).</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">Nuclear fuel cycle</a></div>
<p>A nuclear reactor is only part of the fuel life-cycle for nuclear power. 
The process starts with mining (see <i><a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">Uranium mining</a></i>). 
Uranium mines are underground, <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">open-pit</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_leach" title="In-situ leach">in-situ leach</a> mines. 
In any case, the uranium ore is extracted, usually converted into a stable and compact form such as <a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">yellowcake</a>, and then transported to a processing facility. 
Here, the yellowcake is converted to <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a>, which is then generally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" title="Uranium enrichment">enriched</a> using various techniques. 
Some reactor designs can also use natural uranium without enrichment.
The enriched uranium, containing more than the natural 0.7% U-235, is generally used to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_rod#Common_physical_forms_of_nuclear_fuel" title="Fuel rod">rods</a> of the proper composition and geometry for the particular reactor that the fuel is destined for. 
In modern light-water reactors the fuel rods will spend about 3 operational cycles (typically 6 years total now) inside the reactor, generally until about 3% of their uranium has been fissioned, then they will be moved to a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> where the short lived isotopes generated by fission can decay away. 
After about 5 years in a spent fuel pool the spent fuel is radioactively and thermally cool enough to handle, and it can be moved to dry storage casks or reprocessed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_fuel_resources">Conventional fuel resources</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_market" title="Uranium market">Uranium market</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_development#Nuclear_energy" title="Energy development">Energy development - Nuclear</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="750" height="587" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/200px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/300px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg/400px-Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_enrichment_proportions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proportions of the isotopes, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a> (blue) and uranium-235 (red) found naturally, versus grades that are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enriched_Uranium" title="Enriched Uranium">enriched</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_water_reactor" title="Light water reactor">light water reactors</a> require fuel enriched to (3–4%), while others such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CANDU" title="CANDU">CANDU</a> reactor uses natural uranium.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">Uranium</a> is a fairly common <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> in the Earth's crust: it is approximately as common as <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, and is about 40 times more common than silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> 
Uranium is present in trace concentrations in most rocks, dirt, and ocean water, but can be economically extracted only where it is present in high concentrations. 
Still, as of 2011 the world's measured resources of uranium, economically recoverable at the arbitrary price ceiling of 130 USD/kg, were enough to last for between 70 and 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Red_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Red-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> in 2006, there was an expected 85 years worth of uranium in already identified resources at the then-current utilization rates.  In 2007, the OECD estimated 670 years of economically recoverable uranium in total conventional resources and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> ores assuming the then-current use rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The OECD's red book of 2011 said that known uranium resources had grown by 12.5% since 2008 due to increased exploration, with this increase translating into greater than a century of uranium available if the rate of use were to continue at the 2011 level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2018)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Light water reactors make relatively inefficient use of nuclear fuel, mostly fissioning only the very rare uranium-235 isotope.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a> can make this waste reusable.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
Newer <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">Generation III reactors</a> also achieve a more efficient use of the available resources than the <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">generation II reactors</a> which make up the vast majority of reactors worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-159">[159]</a></sup>
With a pure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_reactor" title="Fast reactor">fast reactor</a> fuel cycle with a burn up of all the Uranium and <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> (which presently make up the most hazardous substances in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a>), there is an estimated 160,000 years worth of Uranium in total conventional resources and phosphate ore at the price of 60–100 US$/kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconventional_fuel_resources">Unconventional fuel resources</span></h4>
<p>Unconventional uranium resources also exist.
Uranium is naturally present in seawater at a concentration of about 3 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">micrograms</a> per liter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.ie_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.ie-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gepr.org_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-gepr.org-165">[165]</a></sup> with 4.5 billion tons of uranium considered present in seawater at any time. 
In 2012 it was estimated that this fuel source could be extracted at 10 times the current price of uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, with the advances made in the efficiency of seawater uranium extraction, it was suggested that it would be economically competitive to produce fuel for light water reactors from seawater if the process was implemented at large scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> 
Uranium extracted on an industrial scale from seawater would constantly be replenished by both river erosion of rocks and the natural process of uranium <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(metallurgy)" title="Leaching (metallurgy)">dissolved</a> from the surface area of the ocean floor, both of which maintain the <a href="/wiki/Solubility_equilibrium" title="Solubility equilibrium">solubility equilibria</a> of seawater concentration at a stable level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gepr.org_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-gepr.org-165">[165]</a></sup> 
Some commentators have argued that this strengthens the case for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power to be considered a renewable energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">Breeder reactor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy</a></div>
<p>As opposed to light water reactors which use uranium-235 (0.7% of all natural uranium), fast breeder reactors use uranium-238 (99.3% of all natural uranium). In 2006 it was estimated that with seawater extraction, there was likely some five billion years' worth of uranium-238 for use in these power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford-cohen_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-cohen-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Breeder technology has been used in several reactors, but the high cost of reprocessing fuel safely, at 2006 technological levels, requires uranium prices of more than 200 USD/kg before becoming justified economically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-anpr_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-anpr-171">[171]</a></sup> 
Breeder reactors are however being pursued as they have the potential to burn up all of the actinides in the present inventory of nuclear waste while also producing power and creating additional quantities of fuel for more reactors via the breeding process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2017, there are only two breeder reactors producing commercial power: the <a href="/wiki/BN-600_reactor" title="BN-600 reactor">BN-600 reactor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/BN-800_reactor" title="BN-800 reactor">BN-800 reactor</a>, both in Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-174">[174]</a></sup>
The BN-600, with a capacity of 600 MW, was built in 1980 in Beloyarsk and is planned to produce power until 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-174">[174]</a></sup>
The BN-800 is an updated version of the BN-600, and started operation in 2014 with a net electrical capacity of 789 MW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-174">[174]</a></sup>
The technical design of a yet larger breeder, the <a href="/wiki/BN-1200_reactor" title="BN-1200 reactor">BN-1200 reactor</a> was originally scheduled to be finalized in 2013, with construction slated for 2015 but has since been delayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ph%C3%A9nix" title="Phénix">Phénix</a> breeder reactor in France was powered down in 2009 after 36 years of operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_174-3"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-174">[174]</a></sup>
Japan's <a href="/wiki/Monju_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Monju Nuclear Power Plant">Monju</a> breeder reactor restarted (having been shut down in 1995) in 2010 for 3 months, but shut down again after equipment fell into the reactor during reactor checkups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
The reactor was decommissioned in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNAfast_174-4"><a href="#cite_note-WNAfast-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Both China and India are building breeder reactors. 
The Indian 500 MWe <a href="/wiki/Prototype_Fast_Breeder_Reactor" title="Prototype Fast Breeder Reactor">Prototype Fast Breeder Reactor</a> is in the commissioning phase,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> with plans to build five more by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> 
The <a href="/wiki/China_Experimental_Fast_Reactor" title="China Experimental Fast Reactor">China Experimental Fast Reactor</a> began operating in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another alternative to fast breeders is thermal breeder reactors that use uranium-233 bred from <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> as fission fuel in the <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium fuel cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-180">[180]</a></sup>
Thorium is about 3.5 times more common than uranium in the Earth's crust, and has different geographic characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-180">[180]</a></sup>
This would extend the total practical fissionable resource base by 450%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_180-2"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-180">[180]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/India%27s_three-stage_nuclear_power_programme" title="India's three-stage nuclear power programme">India's three-stage nuclear power programme</a> features the use of a thorium fuel cycle in the third stage, as it has abundant thorium reserves but little uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-thorium_180-3"><a href="#cite_note-wna-thorium-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_waste">Nuclear waste</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">Radioactive waste</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_waste_treatment_technologies" title="List of nuclear waste treatment technologies">List of nuclear waste treatment technologies</a></div>
<p>The most important waste stream from nuclear power plants is <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a>. 
It is primarily composed of unconverted uranium as well as significant quantities of transuranic actinides (plutonium and <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a>, mostly). 
In addition, about 3% of it is fission products from nuclear reactions. 
The actinides (uranium, plutonium, and curium) are responsible for the bulk of the long-term radioactivity, whereas the fission products are responsible for the bulk of the short-term radioactivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-level_radioactive_waste">High-level radioactive waste</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Reactor-grade_plutonium#Reuse_in_reactors" title="Reactor-grade plutonium">Reactor-grade plutonium § Reuse in reactors</a>, <a href="/wiki/High-level_radioactive_waste_management" title="High-level radioactive waste management">High-level radioactive waste management</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_waste_treatment_technologies" title="List of nuclear waste treatment technologies">List of nuclear waste treatment technologies</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_132603_Nuclear_power_reactor_fuel_assembly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_rod" title="Nuclear fuel rod">nuclear fuel rod</a> assembly bundle being inspected before entering a reactor.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1139" data-file-width="1800" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/220px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/330px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg/440px-Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_dry_storage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Following interim storage in a <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a>, the bundles of used fuel assemblies of a typical nuclear power station are often stored on site in the likes of the eight <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> vessels pictured above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Rowe_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station">Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station</a>, which generated 44 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hours" title="Kilowatt hours">kilowatt hours</a> of electricity over its lifetime, its complete spent fuel inventory is contained within sixteen casks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>High-level radioactive waste management concerns management and disposal of highly radioactive materials created during production of nuclear power. The technical issues in accomplishing this are daunting, due to the extremely long periods <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_waste" title="Radioactive waste">radioactive wastes</a> remain deadly to living organisms. Of particular concern are two <a href="/wiki/Long-lived_fission_product" title="Long-lived fission product">long-lived fission products</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technetium-99" title="Technetium-99">Technetium-99</a> (half-life 220,000 years) and <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">Iodine-129</a> (half-life 15.7 million years),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> which dominate spent nuclear fuel radioactivity after a few thousand years. The most troublesome <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element" title="Transuranium element">transuranic elements</a> in spent fuel are <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_neptunium" title="Isotopes of neptunium">Neptunium-237</a> (half-life two million years) and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">Plutonium-239</a> (half-life 24,000 years).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Consequently, high-level radioactive waste requires sophisticated treatment and management to successfully isolate it from the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. This usually necessitates treatment, followed by a long-term management strategy involving permanent storage, disposal or transformation of the waste into a non-toxic form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>Governments around the world are considering a range of waste management and disposal options, usually involving deep-geologic placement, although there has been limited progress toward implementing long-term waste management solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> This is partly because the timeframes in question when dealing with radioactive waste range from 10,000 to millions of years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> according to studies based on the effect of estimated radiation doses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some proposed nuclear reactor designs however such as the American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integral_Fast_Reactor" title="Integral Fast Reactor">Integral Fast Reactor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_reactor" title="Molten salt reactor">Molten salt reactor</a> can use the nuclear waste from light water reactors as a fuel, transmutating it to isotopes that would be safe after hundreds, instead of tens of thousands of years. This offers a potentially more attractive alternative to deep geological disposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another possibility is the use of thorium in a reactor especially designed for thorium (rather than mixing in thorium with uranium and plutonium (i.e. in existing reactors). Used thorium fuel remains only a few hundreds of years radioactive, instead of tens of thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the fraction of a radioisotope's atoms decaying per unit of time is inversely proportional to its half-life, the relative radioactivity of a quantity of buried human radioactive waste would diminish over time compared to natural radioisotopes (such as the decay chains of 120 trillion tons of thorium and 40 trillion tons of uranium which are <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">at relatively trace concentrations of parts per million each</a> over the crust's 3 * 10<sup>19</sup> ton mass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> For instance, over a timeframe of thousands of years, after the most active short half-life radioisotopes decayed, burying U.S. nuclear waste would increase the radioactivity in the top 2000 feet of rock and soil in the United States (10 million km<sup>2</sup>) by <a href="/wiki/Approximation#Mathematics" title="Approximation">≈</a> 1 part in 10 million over the cumulative amount of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_radioactivity" title="Natural radioactivity">natural radioisotopes</a> in such a volume, although the vicinity of the site would have a far higher concentration of artificial radioisotopes underground than such an average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Low-level_radioactive_waste">Low-level radioactive waste</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Low-level_waste" title="Low-level waste">Low-level waste</a></div>
<p>The nuclear industry also produces a large volume of low-level radioactive waste in the form of contaminated items like clothing, hand tools, water purifier resins, and (upon decommissioning) the materials of which the reactor itself is built. 
Low-level waste is generally stored on-site until radiation levels are low enough to be disposed as ordinary waste.
Occasionally low-level waste can be sent to a low-level waste disposal site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_relative_to_other_types">Waste relative to other types</span></h4>
<p>In countries with nuclear power, radioactive wastes account for less than 1% of total industrial toxic wastes, much of which remains hazardous for long periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wna-wmitnfc_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-wna-wmitnfc-159">[159]</a></sup> Overall, nuclear power produces far less waste material by volume than fossil-fuel based power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Coal-burning plants are particularly noted for producing large amounts of toxic and mildly radioactive ash due to concentrating naturally occurring metals and mildly radioactive material from the coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> A 2008 report from <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> concluded that coal power actually results in more radioactivity being released into the environment than nuclear power operation, and that the population <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effective_dose_(radiation_safety)" title="Effective dose (radiation safety)">effective dose</a> equivalent, or dose to the public from radiation from coal plants is 100 times as much as from the operation of nuclear plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-colmain_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-colmain-202">[202]</a></sup> 
Although coal ash is much less radioactive than spent nuclear fuel on a weight per weight basis, coal ash is produced in much higher quantities per unit of energy generated, and this is released directly into the environment as <a href="/wiki/Fly_ash" title="Fly ash">fly ash</a>, whereas nuclear plants use shielding to protect the environment from radioactive materials, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Dry_cask_storage" title="Dry cask storage">dry cask storage</a> vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cejournal_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-cejournal-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_disposal">Waste disposal</span></h4>
<p>Disposal of nuclear waste is often said to be the among the most problematic aspects of the industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mont2011_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-mont2011-204">[204]</a></sup> 
Presently, waste is mainly stored at individual reactor sites and there are over 430 locations around the world where radioactive material continues to accumulate. 
Some experts suggest that centralized underground repositories which are well-managed, guarded, and monitored, would be a vast improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mont2011_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-mont2011-204">[204]</a></sup> 
There is an "international consensus on the advisability of storing nuclear waste in <a href="/wiki/Deep_geological_repository" title="Deep geological repository">deep geological repositories</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-go_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-go-205">[205]</a></sup> with the lack of movement of nuclear waste in the 2 billion year old <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">natural nuclear fission reactors</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a> being cited as "a source of essential information today."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WIPP-04.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="192" data-file-width="288" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/WIPP-04.jpeg/220px-WIPP-04.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/WIPP-04.jpeg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WIPP-04.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The placement of waste containers, generated during US cold war activities, underground at the WIPP facility. The facility is seen as a potential pilot plant for later civilian generated, spent fuel.</div></div></div>
<p>There are no commercial scale purpose built underground repositories in operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-go_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-go-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greeninc_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-greeninc-107">[107]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Waste_Isolation_Pilot_Plant" title="Waste Isolation Pilot Plant">Waste Isolation Pilot Plant</a> (WIPP) in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> has been taking nuclear waste since 1999 from production reactors, but as the name suggests is a research and development facility. 
In 2014 a radiation leak caused by violations in the use of chemically reactive packaging<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> brought renewed attention to the need for quality control management, along with some initial calls for more R&amp;D into the alternative methods of disposal for radioactive waste and spent fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeff_Tollefson_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeff_Tollefson-211">[211]</a></sup> 
In 2017, the facility was formally reopened after three years of investigation and cleanup, with the resumption of new storage taking place later that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reprocessing">Reprocessing</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a></div>
<p>Reprocessing can potentially recover up to 95% of the remaining uranium and plutonium in spent nuclear fuel, putting it into new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed_oxide_fuel" title="Mixed oxide fuel">mixed oxide fuel</a>. This produces a reduction in long term radioactivity within the remaining waste, since this is largely short-lived fission products, and reduces its volume by over 90%. Reprocessing of civilian fuel from power reactors is currently done in Europe, Russia, Japan, and India. The full potential of reprocessing had not been achieved as of 2013 because it requires breeder reactors, which were not commercially available at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berrytoll_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE_Spectrum_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE_Spectrum-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear reprocessing reduces the volume of high-level waste, but by itself does not reduce radioactivity or heat generation and therefore does not eliminate the need for a geological waste repository. Reprocessing has been politically controversial because of the potential to contribute to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, the potential vulnerability to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a>, the political challenges of repository siting (a problem that applies equally to direct disposal of spent fuel), and because of its high cost compared to the once-through fuel cycle. Several different methods for reprocessing been tried, but many have had safety and practicality problems which have led to their discontinuation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berrytoll_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-berrytoll-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bas2011_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-bas2011-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the Obama administration stepped back from President Bush's plans for commercial-scale reprocessing and reverted to a program focused on reprocessing-related scientific research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> Reprocessing is not allowed in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.com_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature.com-218">[218]</a></sup> In the United States, spent nuclear fuel is currently all treated as waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> A major recommendation of the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America%27s_Nuclear_Future" title="Blue Ribbon Commission on America's Nuclear Future">Blue Ribbon Commission on America's Nuclear Future</a> was that "the United States should undertake an integrated nuclear waste management program that leads to the timely development of one or more permanent deep geological facilities for the safe disposal of spent fuel and high-level nuclear waste".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America’s_Nuclear_Future_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Ribbon_Commission_on_America’s_Nuclear_Future-220">[220]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Depleted_uranium">Depleted uranium</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">Depleted uranium</a></div>
<p>Uranium enrichment produces large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">depleted uranium</a> (DU), which consists of U-238 with most of the easily fissile U-235 isotope removed. 
U-238 is a tough metal with several commercial uses including aircraft production, radiation shielding, and armor, as it has a higher density than lead. 
Depleted uranium is also controversially used in munitions; DU penetrators (bullets or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/APFSDS" title="APFSDS">APFSDS</a> tips) "self sharpen", due to uranium's tendency to fracture along shear bands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Accidents.2C_attacks_and_safety"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents,_attacks_and_safety">Accidents, attacks and safety</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents">Accidents</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="1095" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/220px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/330px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/440px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Following the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>, the world's worst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" title="Nuclear accident">nuclear accident</a> since 1986, 50,000 households were displaced after <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> leaked into the air, soil and sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> Radiation checks led to bans of some shipments of vegetables and fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">Energy accidents</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety">Nuclear safety</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents">Nuclear and radiation accidents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_nuclear_disasters_and_radioactive_incidents" title="Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents">Lists of nuclear disasters and radioactive incidents</a></div>
<p>Some serious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents_by_death_toll" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents by death toll">nuclear and radiation accidents</a> have occurred. 
The severity of nuclear accidents is generally classified using the <a href="/wiki/International_Nuclear_Event_Scale" title="International Nuclear Event Scale">International Nuclear Event Scale</a> (INES) introduced by the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">International Atomic Energy Agency</a> (IAEA).
The scale ranks anomalous events or accidents on a scale from 0 (a deviation from normal operation that pose no safety risk) to 7 (a major accident with widespread effects). 
There have been 3 accidents of level 5 or higher in the civilian nuclear power industry, two of which, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chernobyl_accident" title="Chernobyl accident">Chernobyl accident</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_accident" title="Fukushima accident">Fukushima accident</a>, are ranked at level 7.
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chernobyl_accident" title="Chernobyl accident">Chernobyl accident</a> in 1986 caused approximately 50 deaths from direct and indirect effects, and many more serious injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2012_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2012-225">[225]</a></sup>
The exact death toll due to indirect effects of the widespread radiation contamination is difficult to measure, and depends on the assumptions used. 
Some studies put the total death toll of the Chernobyl accident from indirect effects much higher, in the order of thousands of people.
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_accident" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident</a> was caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Tohoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tohoku earthquake and tsunami">2011 Tohoku earthquake and tsunami</a>. 
The accident has not caused any radiation related deaths, but resulted in radioactive contamination of surrounding areas.
The difficult <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_disaster_cleanup" title="Fukushima disaster cleanup">Fukushima disaster cleanup</a> will take 40 or more years, and is expected to cost tens of billions of dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richard_Schiffman_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Schiffman-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Fackler_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Fackler-227">[227]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> in 1979 was a smaller scale accident, rated at INES level 5. 
There were no direct or indirect deaths caused by the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timenuke_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other less serious accidents are more common. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_K._Sovacool" title="Benjamin K. Sovacool">Benjamin K. Sovacool</a> has reported that worldwide there have been 99 accidents (defined as either resulting in loss of human life or more than $50,000 of property damage) at nuclear power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critev_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-critev-228">[228]</a></sup> 
Fifty-seven accidents have occurred since the Chernobyl disaster, and 57% (56 out of 99) of all nuclear-related accidents have occurred in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-critev_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-critev-228">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>Military nuclear-powered submarine mishaps include the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-19" title="Soviet submarine K-19">K-19</a> reactor accident (1961),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rad_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-rad-33">[33]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-27" title="Soviet submarine K-27">K-27</a> reactor accident (1968),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnston2007_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-34">[34]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_submarine_K-431" title="Soviet submarine K-431">K-431</a> reactor accident (1985).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timenuke_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-35">[35]</a></sup> 
International research is continuing into safety improvements such as passively safe plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Baurac_2002_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_Baurac_2002-229">[229]</a></sup> and the possible future use of nuclear fusion.
</p><p>According to Benjamin K. Sovacool, fission energy accidents ranked first among energy sources in terms of their total economic cost, accounting for 41 percent of all property damage attributed to energy accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> 
Another analysis presented in the international journal <i>Human and Ecological Risk Assessment</i> found that coal, oil, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_petroleum_gas" title="Liquid petroleum gas">Liquid petroleum gas</a> and hydroelectric accidents (primarily due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banqiao_dam" title="Banqiao dam">Banqiao dam</a> burst) have resulted in greater economic impacts than nuclear power accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tandfonline1_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline1-231">[231]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h3>
<p>In terms of lives lost per unit of energy generated, nuclear power has caused fewer accidental deaths per unit of energy generated than all other major sources of energy generation. 
Energy produced by coal, petroleum, natural gas and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> has caused more deaths per unit of energy generated due to air pollution and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a>. 
This is found when comparing the immediate deaths from other energy sources to both the immediate nuclear related deaths from accidents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-232">[232]</a></sup> and also including the latent, or predicted, indirect cancer deaths from nuclear <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accidents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theage2006_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-theage2006-233">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tandfonline1_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline1-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> 
When the combined immediate and indirect fatalities from nuclear power and all fossil fuels are compared, including fatalities resulting from the mining of the necessary natural resources to power generation and to air pollution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-9">[9]</a></sup> the use of nuclear power has been calculated to have prevented about 1.8 million deaths between 1971 and 2009, by reducing the proportion of energy that would otherwise have been generated by fossil fuels, and is projected to continue to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-235">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
Following the 2011 Fukushima nuclear disaster, it has been estimated that if Japan had never adopted nuclear power, accidents and pollution from coal or gas plants would have caused more lost years of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup>
</p><p>Forced evacuation from a nuclear accident may lead to social isolation, anxiety, depression, psychosomatic medical problems, reckless behavior, even suicide. 
Such was the outcome of the 1986 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chernobyl_nuclear_disaster" title="Chernobyl nuclear disaster">Chernobyl nuclear disaster</a> in Ukraine. 
A comprehensive 2005 study concluded that "the mental health impact of Chernobyl is the largest public health problem unleashed by the accident to date".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riv12_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-riv12-238">[238]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Frank_N._von_Hippel" title="Frank N. von Hippel">Frank N. von Hippel</a>, an American scientist, commented on the 2011 Fukushima nuclear disaster, saying that a disproportionate <a href="/wiki/Radiophobia" title="Radiophobia">radiophobia</a>, or "fear of ionizing radiation could have long-term psychological effects on a large portion of the population in the contaminated areas".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frank_N._von_Hippel_27–36_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_N._von_Hippel_27–36-239">[239]</a></sup> 
A 2015 report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">Lancet</a></i> explained that serious impacts of nuclear accidents were often not directly attributable to radiation exposure, but rather social and psychological effects. 
Evacuation and long-term displacement of affected populations created problems for many people, especially the elderly and hospital patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup>
As of 2013, about 160,000 evacuees still live in temporary housing due to the evacuation order following the Fukushima accident.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attacks_and_sabotage">Attacks and sabotage</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_of_nuclear_plants_to_attack" title="Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack">Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">Nuclear terrorism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_in_the_United_States" title="Nuclear safety in the United States">Nuclear safety in the United States</a></div>
<p>Terrorists could target <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a> in an attempt to release <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radioactive contamination</a> into the community. The United States 9/11 Commission has said that nuclear power plants were potential targets originally considered for the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a>. An attack on a reactor’s <a href="/wiki/Spent_fuel_pool" title="Spent fuel pool">spent fuel pool</a> could also be serious, as these pools are less protected than the reactor core. The release of radioactivity could lead to thousands of near-term deaths and greater numbers of long-term fatalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas12_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-241">[241]</a></sup>
</p><p>If nuclear power use is to expand significantly, nuclear facilities will have to be made extremely safe from attacks that could release massive quantities of radioactivity. 
New reactor designs have features of <a href="/wiki/Passive_nuclear_safety" title="Passive nuclear safety">passive safety</a>, such as the flooding of the reactor core without active intervention by reactor operators. 
However, these safety measures have generally been developed and studied with respect to accidents, not to the deliberate reactor attack by a terrorist group. 
The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> (NRC) does now also require new reactor license applications to consider security during the design stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas12_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-241">[241]</a></sup> 
In the United States, the NRC carries out "Force on Force" (FOF) exercises at all Nuclear Power Plant (NPP) sites at least once every three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas12_241-2"><a href="#cite_note-fas12-241">[241]</a></sup> 
In the United States, plants are surrounded by a double row of tall fences which are electronically monitored. 
The plant grounds are patrolled by a sizeable force of armed guards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insider sabotage is also a threat because insiders can observe and work around security measures. 
Successful insider crimes depended on the perpetrators' observation and knowledge of security vulnerabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> 
A fire caused 5–10 million dollars worth of damage to New York's <a href="/wiki/Indian_Point_Energy_Center" title="Indian Point Energy Center">Indian Point Energy Center</a> in 1971. 
The arsonist turned out to be a plant maintenance worker. 
Sabotage by workers has been reported at many other reactors in the United States: at <a href="/wiki/Zion_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Zion Nuclear Power Station">Zion Nuclear Power Station</a> (1974), <a href="/wiki/Quad_Cities_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Quad Cities Nuclear Generating Station">Quad Cities Nuclear Generating Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peach_Bottom_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Peach Bottom Nuclear Generating Station">Peach Bottom Nuclear Generating Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fort_St._Vrain_Generating_Station" title="Fort St. Vrain Generating Station">Fort St. Vrain Generating Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trojan_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Trojan Nuclear Power Plant">Trojan Nuclear Power Plant</a> (1974), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Browns_Ferry_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Browns Ferry Nuclear Power Plant">Browns Ferry Nuclear Power Plant</a> (1980), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beaver_Valley_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Beaver Valley Nuclear Generating Station">Beaver Valley Nuclear Generating Station</a> (1981). Many reactors overseas have also reported sabotage by workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amory_Lovins_2001_145–146_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amory_Lovins_2001_145–146-244">[244]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="555" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/220px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/330px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/440px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>United States and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a>/Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> stockpiles, 1945-2006.The <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a> was the main driving force behind the sharp reduction in the quantity of nuclear weapons worldwide since the cold war ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-245">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usec.com_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-usec.com-246">[246]</a></sup> However without an increase in nuclear reactors and greater demand for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">fissile</a> fuel, the cost of dismantling has dissuaded Russia from continuing their disarmament.</div></div></div>
<p>Many technologies and materials associated with the creation of a nuclear power program have a dual-use capability, in that they can be used to make <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a> if a country chooses to do so. When this happens a nuclear power program can become a route leading to a nuclear weapon or a public annex to a "secret" weapons program. The concern over <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_program_of_Iran" title="Nuclear program of Iran">Iran's nuclear activities</a> is a case in point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dfall2009_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-dfall2009-247">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of April 2012 there were <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">thirty one countries</a> that have civil nuclear power plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> of which <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">nine have nuclear weapons</a>, with the vast majority of these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_state" title="Nuclear weapons state">nuclear weapons states</a> having first produced weapons, before commercial fission electricity stations. 
Moreover, the re-purposing of civilian nuclear industries for military purposes would be a breach of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-proliferation_treaty" title="Non-proliferation treaty">Non-proliferation treaty</a>, of which 190 countries adhere to.
</p><p>A fundamental goal for global security is to minimize the nuclear proliferation risks associated with the expansion of nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dfall2009_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-dfall2009-247">[247]</a></sup> 
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_Nuclear_Energy_Partnership" title="Global Nuclear Energy Partnership">Global Nuclear Energy Partnership</a> is an international effort to create a distribution network in which developing countries in need of energy would receive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> at a discounted rate, in exchange for that nation agreeing to forgo their own indigenous develop of a uranium enrichment program. 
The France-based <a href="/wiki/Eurodif" title="Eurodif">Eurodif</a>/<i>European Gaseous Diffusion Uranium Enrichment Consortium</i> is a program that successfully implemented this concept, with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Spain" title="Nuclear power in Spain">Spain</a> and other countries without enrichment facilities buying a share of the fuel produced at the French controlled enrichment facility, but without a transfer of technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> 
Iran was an early participant from 1974, and remains a shareholder of Eurodif via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sofidif" title="Sofidif">Sofidif</a>.
</p><p>According to Benjamin K. Sovacool, a "number of high-ranking officials, even within the United Nations, have argued that they can do little to stop states using nuclear reactors to produce nuclear weapons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bks2011_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-bks2011-250">[250]</a></sup> 
A 2009 United Nations report said that:
</p>
<blockquote><p>the revival of interest in nuclear power could result in the worldwide dissemination of uranium enrichment and spent fuel reprocessing technologies, which present obvious risks of proliferation as these technologies can produce fissile materials that are directly usable in nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bks2011_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-bks2011-250">[250]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>On the other hand, power reactors can also reduce nuclear weapons arsenals when military grade nuclear materials are reprocessed to be used as fuel in nuclear power plants. 
The <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a>, the brainchild of Thomas Neff of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014-251">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> is the single most successful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-proliferation" title="Non-proliferation">non-proliferation</a> program to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-245">[245]</a></sup> 
Up to 2005, the Megatons to Megawatts Program had processed $8 billion of high enriched, weapons grade uranium into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_enriched_uranium" title="Low enriched uranium">low enriched uranium</a> suitable as nuclear fuel for commercial fission reactors by diluting it with <a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">natural uranium</a>.
This corresponds to the elimination of 10,000 nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup>
For approximately two decades, this material generated nearly 10 percent of all the electricity consumed in the United States (about half of all U.S. nuclear electricity generated) with a total of around 7 trillion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hours" title="Kilowatt-hours">kilowatt-hours</a> of electricity produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-254">[254]</a></sup> Enough energy to energize the entire United States electric grid for about two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014-251">[251]</a></sup> In total it is estimated to have cost $17 billion, a "bargain for US ratepayers", with Russia profiting $12 billion from the deal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_254-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-254">[254]</a></sup> Much needed profit for the <a href="/wiki/Rosatom" title="Rosatom">Russian nuclear oversight industry</a>, which after the collapse of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_economy" title="Soviet economy">Soviet economy</a>, had difficulties paying for the maintenance and security of the Russian Federations highly enriched uranium and warheads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014_251-2"><a href="#cite_note-A_Farewell_to_Arms,_2014-251">[251]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Megatons to Megawatts Program was hailed as a major success by anti-nuclear weapon advocates as it has largely been the driving force behind the sharp reduction in the quantity of nuclear weapons worldwide since the cold war ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebulletin.org_245-2"><a href="#cite_note-thebulletin.org-245">[245]</a></sup> 
However without an increase in nuclear reactors and greater demand for fissile fuel, the cost of dismantling and down blending has dissuaded Russia from continuing their disarmament.
As of 2013 Russia appears to not be interested in extending the program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact<span id="Environmental_issues"></span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_nuclear_power" title="Environmental impact of nuclear power">Environmental impact of nuclear power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="349" data-file-width="807" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png/220px-Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png/330px-Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png/440px-Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Power_Plant_Cattenom_a.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel_power_station" title="Fossil-fuel power station">fossil fuel power plants</a>, the only substance leaving the cooling towers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plants" title="Nuclear power plants">nuclear power plants</a> is non-radioactive water vapour and thus does not <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">pollute the air</a> or cause <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_emissions">Carbon emissions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="944" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png/220px-Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png/330px-Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png/440px-Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sovacool_2008_life-cycle_study.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 2008 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meta_analysis" title="Meta analysis">meta analysis</a> of 103 studies, published by Benjamin K. Sovacool, estimated that the <i><a href="/wiki/Mean" title="Mean">mean</a></i> value of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions for nuclear power over the lifecycle of a plant was 66.08 g/kW·h, <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources#2008_Benjamin_K._Sovacool_survey_of_nuclear_power" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">this 2008 study by Sovacool</a> garnered controversy by comparing statistical mean with median values,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beerten_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beerten-256">[256]</a></sup> to suggest various <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> sources were "two to seven times more effective at combating climate change", by presenting <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> values of "9–32 g/kW·h" for the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sov_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-sov-257">[257]</a></sup> A similar 2012 meta-analysis by <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> arrived at a different conclusion, with the <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> value, depending on which reactor design was analyzed, ranging from 11 to 25 g/kW·h of total life cycle nuclear power CO<sub>2</sub> emissions, a figure comparable, and in some instances lower, than a number of renewable energy generation methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup></div></div></div>
