<p>Nuclear power is one of the leading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power_generation" title="Low carbon power generation">low carbon power generation</a> methods of producing <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, and in terms of <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">total life-cycle greenhouse gas emissions per unit of energy generated</a>, has emission values comparable to or lower than <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nrel.gov_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nrel.gov-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup>
A 2014 analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a> literature by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) reported that the embodied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" title="Life cycle assessment">total life-cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">emission intensity</a> of fission electricity has a median value of 12 g CO<sub>2</sub><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">eq</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kWh</a> which is the lowest out of all commercial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baseload" title="Baseload">baseload</a> energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-260">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-261">[261]</a></sup>
This is contrasted with coal and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_gas" title="Fossil gas">fossil gas</a> at 820 and 490 g CO<sub>2</sub> eq/kWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2014_Annex_III_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2014_Annex_III-260">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-report.mitigation2014.org_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-report.mitigation2014.org-261">[261]</a></sup> 
From the beginning of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_station" title="Nuclear power station">fission-electric power station</a> commercialization in the 1970s, nuclear power prevented the emission of about 64 billion tonnes of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalent</a> that would have otherwise resulted from the burning of fossil fuels in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kharecha_Pushker_A_2013_4889–4895-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation">Radiation</span></h3>
<p>According to the United Nations (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNSCEAR" title="UNSCEAR">UNSCEAR</a>), regular nuclear power plant operation including the nuclear fuel cycle causes radioisotope releases into the environment amounting to 0.0002 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millisievert" title="Millisievert">millisieverts</a> (mSv) per year of public exposure as a global average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-262">[262]</a></sup> 
This is small compared to variation in natural <a href="/wiki/Background_radiation" title="Background radiation">background radiation</a>, which averages 2.4 mSv/a globally but frequently varies between 1 mSv/a and 13 mSv/a depending on a person's location as determined by UNSCEAR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-262">[262]</a></sup> 
As of a 2008 report, the remaining legacy of the worst nuclear power plant accident (Chernobyl) is 0.002 mSv/a in global average exposure (a figure which was 0.04 mSv per person averaged over the entire populace of the Northern Hemisphere in the year of the accident in 1986, although far higher among the most affected local populations and recovery workers).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_262-2"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-262">[262]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_heat">Waste heat</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_nuclear_power#Waste_heat" title="Environmental impact of nuclear power">Environmental impact of nuclear power § Waste heat</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> causing weather extremes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_waves" title="Heat waves">heat waves</a>, reduced precipitation levels and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Droughts" title="Droughts">droughts</a> can have a significant impact on all <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power station</a> infrastructure, including large biomass-electric and fission-electric stations alike, if cooling in these power stations, namely in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steam_condenser" title="Steam condenser">steam condenser</a> is provided by certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-263">[263]</a></sup> While many thermal stations use indirect seawater cooling or <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling towers</a> that in comparison use little to no freshwater, those that were designed to <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat exchange</a> with rivers and lakes, can run into economic problems.
</p><p>This presently infrequent generic problem may become increasingly significant over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_263-1"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-263">[263]</a></sup> This can force nuclear reactors to be shut down, as happened in France during the 2003 and 2006 heat waves. Nuclear power supply was severely diminished by low river flow rates and droughts, which meant rivers had reached the maximum temperatures for cooling reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_263-2"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-263">[263]</a></sup> During the heat waves, 17 reactors had to limit output or shut down. 77% of French electricity is produced by nuclear power and in 2009 a similar situation created a 8GW shortage and forced the French government to import electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_263-3"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-263">[263]</a></sup> Other cases have been reported from Germany, where extreme temperatures have reduced nuclear power production only 9 times due to high temperatures between 1979 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODI1_263-4"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-263">[263]</a></sup>
</p><p>If global warming continues, this disruption is likely to increase or alternatively, station operators could instead retro-fit other means of cooling, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooling_towers" title="Cooling towers">cooling towers</a>, despite these frequently being large structures and therefore sometimes unpopular with the public.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_renewable_energy">Comparison with renewable energy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_debate" title="Renewable energy debate">Renewable energy debate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_proposed_as_renewable_energy" title="Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy">Nuclear power proposed as renewable energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_electricity_by_source" title="Cost of electricity by source">Cost of electricity by source</a></div>
<p>There is an ongoing debate on the relative benefits of nuclear power compared to renewable energy sources for the generation of low-carbon electricity.
Proponents of renewable energy argue that wind power and solar power are already cheaper and safer than nuclear power.
Nuclear power proponents argue that renewable energy sources such as wind and solar do not offer the scalability necessary for a large scale decarbonization of the electric grid, mainly due to their intermittency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kloor_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kloor-264">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup>
Although the majority of installed renewable energy across the world is currently in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydro_power" title="Hydro power">hydro power</a>, solar and wind power are growing at a much higher pace, especially in developed countries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Several studies report that it is in principle possible to cover most of energy generation with renewable sources.
The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) has said that if governments were supportive, and the full complement of renewable energy technologies were deployed, renewable energy supply could account for almost 80% of the world's energy use within forty years with a necessary investment in renewables of about 1% of global GDP annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipccccc_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipccccc-266">[266]</a></sup>
This approach could contain greenhouse gas levels to less than 450 parts per million, the safe level beyond which climate change becomes catastrophic and irreversible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipccccc_266-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipccccc-266">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, other studies suggest that solar and wind energy are not cost-effective compared to nuclear power.
The <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> published <i>The Net Benefits of Low and No-Carbon Electricity Technologies</i> in 2014 which states, after performing an energy and emissions cost analysis, that "The net benefits of new nuclear, hydro, and natural gas combined cycle plants far outweigh the net benefits of new wind or solar plants", with the most cost effective low carbon power technology being determined to be nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear power is also proposed as a tested and practical way to implement a low-carbon energy infrastructure, as opposed to renewable sources.
Analysis in 2015 by Professor and Chair of Environmental Sustainability <a href="/wiki/Barry_Brook_(scientist)" title="Barry Brook (scientist)">Barry W. Brook</a> and his colleagues on the topic of replacing fossil fuels entirely, from the electric grid of the world, has determined that at the historically modest and proven-rate at which nuclear energy was added to and replaced fossil fuels in France and Sweden during each nation's building programs in the 1980s, nuclear energy could displace or remove fossil fuels from the electric grid completely within 10 years, "allow[ing] the world to meet the most stringent greenhouse-gas mitigation targets.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-journals.plos.org_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-journals.plos.org-270">[270]</a></sup> 
In a similar analysis, Brook had earlier determined that 50% of all <a href="/wiki/World_energy_consumption" title="World energy consumption">global energy</a>, that is not solely electricity, but transportation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synfuels" title="Synfuels">synfuels</a> etc. could be generated within approximately 30 years, if the global nuclear fission build rate was identical to each of these nation's already proven installation rates in units of installed <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> per year, per unit of global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">GDP</a> (GW/year/$).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-271">[271]</a></sup>
This is in contrast to the conceptual studies for a <i><a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a></i> world, which would require an orders of magnitude more costly global investment per year, which has no historical precedent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-272">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> along with far greater land that would have to be devoted to the wind, wave and solar projects, and the inherent assumption that humanity will use less, and not more, energy in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-271">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-272">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-274">[274]</a></sup>
As Brook notes, the "principal limitations on nuclear fission are not technical, economic or fuel-related, but are instead linked to complex issues of societal acceptance, fiscal and political inertia, and inadequate critical evaluation of the real-world constraints facing [the other] low-carbon alternatives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_271-2"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-271">[271]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several studies conclude that wind and solar power have costs that are comparable or lower than nuclear power, when considering price per kWh.
The cost of constructing established nuclear power reactor designs has followed an increasing trend due to <a href="/wiki/Regulation" title="Regulation">regulations</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Court_cases" title="Court cases">court cases</a> whereas the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LCOE" title="LCOE">levelized cost of electricity</a> (LCOE) is declining for wind and solar power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup> 
In 2010 a report from Solar researchers at Duke University found that solar power may be already cheaper than new nuclear power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncwarn_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncwarn-277">[277]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (October 2017)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> 
However they state that if subsidies were removed for solar power, the crossover point would be delayed by years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup>
Data from the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> (EIA) in 2011 estimated that in 2016, solar will have a levelized cost of electricity almost twice as expensive as nuclear (21¢/kWh for solar, 11.39¢/kWh for nuclear), and wind somewhat less expensive than nuclear (9.7¢/kWh).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup>
However, the EIA has also cautioned that levelized costs of intermittent sources such as wind and solar are not directly comparable to costs of "dispatchable" sources (those that can be adjusted to meet demand), as intermittent sources need costly large-scale back-up power supplies for when the weather changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2010 study by the Global Subsidies Initiative compared global relative <a href="/wiki/Energy_subsidies" title="Energy subsidies">energy subsidies</a>, or government financial aid for the deployment of different energy sources.
Results show that fossil fuels receive about 1 U.S. cents per kWh of energy they produce, nuclear energy receives 1.7 cents / kWh, renewable energy (excluding hydroelectricity) receives 5.0 cents / kWh and biofuels receive 5.1 cents / kWh in subsidies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear power is comparable to, and in some cases lower, than many renewable energy sources in terms of lives lost per unit of electricity delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarkandyaWilkinson2007_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-MarkandyaWilkinson2007-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-232">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starfelt_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starfelt-283">[283]</a></sup>
However, as opposed to renewable energy, conventional designs for nuclear reactors produce intensely radioactive spent fuel that needs to be stored or reprocessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup>
A nuclear plant also needs to be disassembled and removed and much of the disassembled nuclear plant needs to be stored as low level nuclear waste for a few decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_decommissioning">Nuclear decommissioning</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">nuclear decommissioning</a></div>
<p>The financial costs of every nuclear power plant continues for some time after the facility has finished generating its last useful electricity. Once no longer economically viable, nuclear reactors and uranium enrichment facilities are generally decommissioned, returning the facility and its parts to a safe enough level to be entrusted for other uses, such as <a href="/wiki/Greenfield_status" title="Greenfield status">greenfield status</a>. 
After a cooling-off period that may last decades, reactor core materials are dismantled and cut into small pieces to be packed in containers for interim storage or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a> experiments. The consensus on how to approach the task is one that is relatively inexpensive, but it has the potential to be hazardous to the natural environment as it presents opportunities for <a href="/wiki/Human_error" title="Human error">human error</a>, accidents or sabotage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sov11_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-sov11-286">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Waste_Policy_Act" title="Nuclear Waste Policy Act">Nuclear Waste Policy Act</a> and Nuclear Decommissioning Trust Fund is legally required, with utilities banking 0.1 to 0.2 cents/kWh during operations to fund future decommissioning. They must report regularly to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> (NRC) on the status of their decommissioning funds. About 70% of the total estimated cost of decommissioning all U.S. nuclear power reactors has already been collected (on the basis of the average cost of $320 million per reactor-steam turbine unit).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States in 2011, there are 13 reactors that had permanently shut down and are in some phase of decommissioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sov11_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-sov11-286">[286]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Yankee_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Connecticut Yankee Nuclear Power Plant">Connecticut Yankee Nuclear Power Plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yankee_Rowe_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station">Yankee Rowe Nuclear Power Station</a> having completed the process in 2006–2007, after ceasing commercial electricity production circa 1992. 
The majority of the 15 years, was used to allow the station to naturally cool-down on its own, which makes the manual disassembly process both safer and cheaper.
Decommissioning at nuclear sites which have experienced a serious accident are the most expensive and time-consuming.
</p><p>Nuclear power works under an <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">insurance</a> framework that limits or structures accident liabilities in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Convention_on_Third_Party_Liability_in_the_Field_of_Nuclear_Energy" title="Paris Convention on Third Party Liability in the Field of Nuclear Energy">Paris convention on nuclear third-party liability</a>, the Brussels supplementary convention, the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_Civil_Liability_for_Nuclear_Damage" title="Vienna Convention on Civil Liability for Nuclear Damage">Vienna convention on civil liability for nuclear damage</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Price%E2%80%93Anderson_Nuclear_Industries_Indemnity_Act" title="Price–Anderson Nuclear Industries Indemnity Act">Price-Anderson Act</a> in the United States.
It is often argued that this potential shortfall in liability represents an external cost not included in the cost of nuclear electricity; but the cost is small, amounting to about 0.1% of the levelized cost of electricity, according to a CBO study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup>
These beyond-regular-insurance costs for worst-case scenarios are not unique to nuclear power, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" title="Hydroelectric power">hydroelectric power</a> plants are similarly not fully insured against a catastrophic event such as the <a href="/wiki/Banqiao_Dam" title="Banqiao Dam">Banqiao Dam</a> disaster, where 11 million people lost their homes and from 30,000 to 200,000 people died, or large <a href="/wiki/Dam_failure" title="Dam failure">dam failures</a> in general. As private insurers base dam insurance premiums on limited scenarios, major disaster insurance in this sector is likewise provided by the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_on_nuclear_power">Debate on nuclear power</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">Nuclear power debate</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">Nuclear energy policy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pro-nuclear_movement" title="Pro-nuclear movement">Pro-nuclear movement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Anti-nuclear movement</a></div>
<p>The nuclear power debate concerns the controversy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jstor.org_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-292">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power-293">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eleven_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-47">[47]</a></sup> which has surrounded the deployment and use of nuclear fission reactors to generate electricity from nuclear fuel for civilian purposes. The debate about nuclear power peaked during the 1970s and 1980s, when it "reached an intensity unprecedented in the history of technology controversies", in some countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marcuse.org_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-marcuse.org-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Proponents of nuclear energy contend that nuclear power is a <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> source that reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a> and increases <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a> by decreasing dependence on imported energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-295">[295]</a></sup> 
Proponents claim that nuclear power produces virtually no conventional air pollution, such as greenhouse gases and smog, in contrast to the main alternative of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel_power_station" title="Fossil-fuel power station">fossil-fuel power stations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup> Nuclear power can produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base-load" title="Base-load">base-load</a> power unlike many renewables which are <a href="/wiki/Intermittent_energy_source" title="Intermittent energy source">intermittent energy sources</a> lacking large-scale and cheap ways of storing energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a> saw oil as a resource that would <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">run out</a>, and proposed nuclear energy as a replacement energy source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup> 
Proponents claim that the risks of storing waste are small and can be further reduced by using the latest technology in newer reactors, and the operational safety record in the Western world is excellent when compared to the other major kinds of power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup>
</p><p>Opponents believe that nuclear power poses many threats to people and the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Share_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-Share-300">[300]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC_WARN_»_Nuclear_Power_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC_WARN_»_Nuclear_Power-301">[301]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturgis_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sturgis-302">[302]</a></sup> 
These threats include the problems of processing, transport and storage of radioactive nuclear waste, the risk of nuclear weapons proliferation and terrorism, as well as health risks and environmental damage from uranium mining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gierec_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-gierec-303">[303]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-protest_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-protest-304">[304]</a></sup> They also contend that reactors themselves are enormously complex machines where many things can and do go wrong; and there have been serious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_accidents" title="Nuclear accidents">nuclear accidents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bksenpol_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-bksenpol-305">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> Critics do not believe that the risks of using nuclear fission as a power source can be fully offset through the development of new technology. In years past, they also argued that when all the energy-intensive stages of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_chain" title="Nuclear fuel chain">nuclear fuel chain</a> are considered, from uranium mining to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">nuclear decommissioning</a>, nuclear power is neither a low-carbon nor an economical electricity source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-markd_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-markd-309">[309]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arguments of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">economics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety">safety</a> are used by both sides of the debate.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_space">Use in space</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Msl-MMRTG.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="327" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Msl-MMRTG.jpg/220px-Msl-MMRTG.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/ba/Msl-MMRTG.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Msl-MMRTG.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Multi-mission_radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Multi-mission radioisotope thermoelectric generator">Multi-mission radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> (MMRTG), used in several space missions such as the <a href="/wiki/Curiosity_(rover)" title="Curiosity (rover)">Curiosity Mars rover</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_space" title="Nuclear power in space">Nuclear power in space</a></div>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> and fusion appear promising for <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">space propulsion</a> applications, generating higher mission velocities with less <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_mass" title="Reaction mass">reaction mass</a>. This is due to the much higher energy density of nuclear reactions: some 7 orders of magnitude (10,000,000 times) more energetic than the chemical reactions which power the current generation of rockets.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">Radioactive decay</a> has been used on a relatively small scale (few kW), mostly to power <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_mission" title="Space mission">space missions</a> and experiments by using <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> such as those developed at <a href="/wiki/Idaho_National_Laboratory#Space_and_Security_Power_Systems_Facility" title="Idaho National Laboratory">Idaho National Laboratory</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advanced_fission_reactor_designs">Advanced fission reactor designs</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">Generation IV reactor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GenIVRoadmap-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="971" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/440px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/660px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/GenIVRoadmap-en.svg/880px-GenIVRoadmap-en.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GenIVRoadmap-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Generation IV roadmap from <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a></div></div></div>
<p>Current fission reactors in operation around the world are <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">second</a> or <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">third generation</a> systems, with most of the first-generation systems having been already retired. 
Research into advanced <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">generation IV reactor</a> types was officially started by the Generation IV International Forum (GIF) based on eight technology goals, including to improve nuclear safety, improve proliferation resistance, minimize waste, improve natural resource utilization, the ability to consume existing nuclear waste in the production of electricity, and decrease the cost to build and run such plants. 
Most of these reactors differ significantly from current operating light water reactors, and are generally not expected to be available for commercial construction before 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup>
</p><p>One disadvantage of any new reactor technology is that safety risks may be greater initially as reactor operators have little experience with the new design. 
Nuclear engineer <a href="/wiki/David_Lochbaum" title="David Lochbaum">David Lochbaum</a> has explained that almost all serious nuclear accidents have occurred with what was at the time the most recent technology. 
He argues that "the problem with new reactors and accidents is twofold: scenarios arise that are impossible to plan for in simulations; and humans make mistakes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-safe_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-safe-311">[311]</a></sup> 
As one director of a U.S. research laboratory put it, "fabrication, construction, operation, and maintenance of new reactors will face a steep learning curve: advanced technologies will have a heightened risk of accidents and mistakes. The technology may be proven, but people are not".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-safe_311-1"><a href="#cite_note-safe-311">[311]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_nuclear_fusion-fission">Hybrid nuclear fusion-fission</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion%E2%80%93fission_hybrid" title="Nuclear fusion–fission hybrid">Nuclear fusion–fission hybrid</a></div>
<p>Hybrid nuclear power is a proposed means of generating power by use of a combination of nuclear fusion and fission processes. The concept dates to the 1950s, and was briefly advocated by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Hans Bethe</a> during the 1970s, but largely remained unexplored until a revival of interest in 2009, due to delays in the realization of pure fusion. When a sustained nuclear fusion power plant is built, it has the potential to be capable of extracting all the fission energy that remains in spent fission fuel, reducing the volume of nuclear waste by orders of magnitude, and more importantly, eliminating all actinides present in the spent fuel, substances which cause security concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hybrid_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-hybrid-312">[312]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_fusion">Nuclear fusion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_(9786811206).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2040" data-file-width="2040" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/220px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/330px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg/440px-U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_%289786811206%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Department_of_Energy_-_Science_-_425_003_001_(9786811206).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of the <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a> <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a> under construction in France.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Fusion power</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Nuclear fusion</a> reactions have the potential to be safer and generate less radioactive waste than fission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[313]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldEnergyCouncil_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-WorldEnergyCouncil-314">[314]</a></sup> 
These reactions appear potentially viable, though technically quite difficult and have yet to be created on a scale that could be used in a functional power plant. 
Fusion power has been under theoretical and experimental investigation since the 1950s.
</p><p>Several experimental nuclear fusion reactors and facilities exist. 
The largest and most ambitious international nuclear fusion project currently in progress is <a href="/wiki/ITER" title="ITER">ITER</a>, a large <a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">tokamak</a> under construction in France.
ITER is planned to pave the way for commercial fusion power by demonstrating self-sustained nuclear fusion reactions with positive energy gain. 
Construction of the ITER facility began in 2007, but the project has run into many delays and budget overruns. 
The facility is now not expected to begin operations until the year 2027 – 11 years after initially anticipated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[315]</a></sup> A follow on commercial nuclear fusion power station, <a href="/wiki/DEMOnstration_Power_Station" title="DEMOnstration Power Station">DEMO</a>, has been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITERorg_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-316">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFDA_Activities_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-EFDA_Activities-317">[317]</a></sup> There are also suggestions for a power plant based upon a different fusion approach, that of an <a href="/wiki/Inertial_fusion_power_plant" title="Inertial fusion power plant">inertial fusion power plant</a>.
</p><p>Fusion powered electricity generation was initially believed to be readily achievable, as fission-electric power had been. However, the extreme requirements for continuous reactions and <a href="/wiki/Plasma_containment" title="Plasma containment">plasma containment</a> led to projections being extended by several decades. In 2010, more than 60 years after the first attempts, commercial power production was still believed to be unlikely before 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITERorg_316-1"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-316">[316]</a></sup>
</p>
